<div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy<br />
Probe (WMAP)</a></div>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Nicolaus Copernicus</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>, <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>-/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <span lang="pl" xml:lang="pl"><i>Mikołaj Kopernik</i></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> German: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Nikolaus Kopernikus; Niklas Koppernigk</i></span>; 19 February 1473&#160;– 24 May 1543) was a <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>-era mathematician and astronomer who formulated a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres#Renaissance" title="Celestial spheres">the universe</a> that placed <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">the Sun rather than the Earth at the center of the universe</a>, likely independently of <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a>, who had formulated such a model some eighteen centuries earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[a]</a></sup></p>
<p>The publication of Copernicus' model in his book <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (<i>On the Revolutions of the Celestial Spheres</i>), just before his death in 1543, was a major event in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a>, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_Revolution" title="Copernican Revolution">Copernican Revolution</a> and making an important contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific Revolution">Scientific Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus was born and died in <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussia</a>, a region that had been part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1385%E2%80%931569)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1385–1569)">Kingdom of Poland</a> since 1466. A <a href="/wiki/Polyglot_(person)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyglot (person)">polyglot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a>, he obtained a doctorate in <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> and was also a <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classics_scholar" class="mw-redirect" title="Classics scholar">classics scholar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">translator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Governor" title="Governor">governor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diplomat" title="Diplomat">diplomat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a>. In 1517 he derived a <a href="/wiki/Quantity_theory_of_money" title="Quantity theory of money">quantity theory of money</a> –&#160;a key concept in economics&#160;– and in 1519 he formulated an economics principle that later came to be called <a href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_law" title="Gresham's law">Gresham's law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-armitage91_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage91-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<p>Nicolaus Copernicus was born on 19 February 1473 in the city of <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Thorn (modern Toruń)</a>, in the province of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussia</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_the_Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Crown of the Kingdom of Poland">Crown of the Kingdom of Poland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dzieje_nauki_polskiej_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dzieje_nauki_polskiej-11">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-plato.stanford_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford-12">[11]</a></sup> His father was a merchant from <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> and his mother was the daughter of a wealthy Toruń merchant.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Nicolaus was the youngest of four children. His brother Andreas (Andrew) became an <a href="/wiki/Augustinian" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustinian">Augustinian</a> <a href="/wiki/Canon_(priest)" title="Canon (priest)">canon</a> at <a href="/wiki/Frombork" title="Frombork">Frombork</a> (Frauenburg).<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> His sister Barbara, named after her mother, became a <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">nun</a> and, in her final years, <a href="/wiki/Prioress" class="mw-redirect" title="Prioress">prioress</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno" title="Chełmno">Chełmno</a> (Kulm); she died after 1517.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> His sister Katharina married the businessman and Toruń city councilor Barthel Gertner and left five children, whom Copernicus looked after to the end of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Copernicus never married and is not known to have had children, but from at least 1531 until 1539 his relations with Anna Schilling, a live-in housekeeper, were seen as scandalous by two bishops of Warmia who urged him over the years to break off relations with his "mistress".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Father's_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Father.27s_family">Father's family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CopernicusHouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CopernicusHouse.jpg/170px-CopernicusHouse.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CopernicusHouse.jpg/255px-CopernicusHouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CopernicusHouse.jpg/340px-CopernicusHouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="2917" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CopernicusHouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Toruń</a> birthplace (ul. Kopernika 15, <i>left</i>). Together with the house at no. 17 (<i>right</i>), it forms the <i>Muzeum Mikołaja Kopernika</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Copernicus' father's family can be traced to a village in <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a> near <a href="/wiki/Nysa,_Poland" title="Nysa, Poland">Nysa</a> (Neiße). The village's name has been variously spelled Kopernik,<sup id="cite_ref-google2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google2-15">[14]</a></sup> Copernik, Copernic, Kopernic, Coprirnik, and today <a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Koperniki</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb3_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb3-16">[15]</a></sup> In the 14th century, members of the family began moving to various other Silesian cities, to the Polish capital, <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> (1367), and to Toruń (1400).<sup id="cite_ref-psb3_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb3-16">[15]</a></sup> The father, Mikołaj the Elder, likely the son of Jan, came from the Kraków line.<sup id="cite_ref-psb3_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb3-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nicolaus was named after his father, who appears in records for the first time as a well-to-do merchant who dealt in copper, selling it mostly in <a href="/wiki/Danzig" class="mw-redirect" title="Danzig">Danzig</a> (Gdańsk).<sup id="cite_ref-google3_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google3-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google4_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google4-18">[17]</a></sup> He moved from Kraków to Toruń around 1458.<sup id="cite_ref-google5_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google5-19">[18]</a></sup> Toruń, situated on the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vistula River">Vistula River</a>, was at that time embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Years%27_War_(1454%E2%80%9366)" title="Thirteen Years' War (1454–66)">Thirteen Years' War</a>, in which the Kingdom of Poland and the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Confederation" title="Prussian Confederation">Prussian Confederation</a>, an alliance of <a href="/wiki/Prussia_(region)" title="Prussia (region)">Prussian</a> cities, gentry and clergy, fought the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> over control of the region. In this war, <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic</a> cities like Danzig and Toruń, Nicolaus Copernicus's hometown, chose to support the <a href="/wiki/Monarchs_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Monarchs of Poland">Polish King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon" title="Casimir IV Jagiellon">Casimir IV Jagiellon</a>, who promised to respect the cities' traditional vast independence, which the Teutonic Order had challenged. Nicolaus' father was actively engaged in the politics of the day and supported Poland and the cities against the Teutonic Order.<sup id="cite_ref-google6_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google6-20">[19]</a></sup> In 1454 he mediated negotiations between Poland's Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Zbigniew_Ole%C5%9Bnicki_(cardinal)" title="Zbigniew Oleśnicki (cardinal)">Zbigniew Oleśnicki</a> and the Prussian cities for repayment of war loans.<sup id="cite_ref-psb3_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb3-16">[15]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Second_Peace_of_Thorn_(1466)" title="Second Peace of Thorn (1466)">Second Peace of Thorn</a> (1466), the Teutonic Order formally relinquished all claims to its western province, which as <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussia</a> remained a region of the Crown of the Kingdom of Poland until the First (1772) and Second (1793) <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a>.</p>
<p>Copernicus's father married Barbara Watzenrode, the astronomer's mother, between 1461 and 1464.<sup id="cite_ref-psb3_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb3-16">[15]</a></sup> He died about 1483.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Mother's_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mother.27s_family">Mother's family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg/170px-%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg/255px-%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="335" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C5%81ukasz_Watzenrode.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copernicus' maternal uncle, <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode_the_Younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucas Watzenrode the Younger">Lucas Watzenrode the Younger</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nicolaus' mother, Barbara Watzenrode, was the daughter of a wealthy Toruń patrician and city councillor, <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode_the_Elder" title="Lucas Watzenrode the Elder">Lucas Watzenrode the Elder</a> (deceased 1462), and Katarzyna (widow of Jan Peckau), mentioned in other sources as <i>Katarzyna Rüdiger gente Modlibóg</i> (deceased 1476).<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> The Modlibógs were a prominent Polish family who had been well known in Poland's history since 1271.<sup id="cite_ref-Stephen_Mizwa_1943.2C_p._38_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephen_Mizwa_1943.2C_p._38-21">[20]</a></sup> The Watzenrode family, like the Kopernik family, had come from Silesia from near <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Awidnica" title="Świdnica">Świdnica</a> (Schweidnitz), and after 1360 had settled in Toruń. They soon became one of the wealthiest and most influential <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(post-Roman_Europe)" title="Patrician (post-Roman Europe)">patrician</a> families.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Through the Watzenrodes' extensive family relationships by marriage, Copernicus was related to wealthy families of Toruń (Thorn), Gdańsk (Danzig) and <a href="/wiki/Elbl%C4%85g" title="Elbląg">Elbląg</a> (Elbing), and to prominent Polish noble families of Prussia: the <a href="/wiki/Czapski" class="mw-redirect" title="Czapski">Czapskis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dzia%C5%82y%C5%84ski" title="Działyński">Działyńskis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Konopacki" class="mw-redirect" title="Konopacki">Konopackis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bcielecki" title="Kościelecki">Kościeleckis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Lucas and Katherine had three children: <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode_the_Younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucas Watzenrode the Younger">Lucas Watzenrode the Younger</a> (1447–1512), who would become <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Warmia">Bishop of Warmia</a> and Copernicus's patron; Barbara, the astronomer's mother (deceased after 1495); and Christina (deceased before 1502), who in 1459 married the Toruń merchant and mayor, Tiedeman von Allen.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lucas Watzenrode the Elder, a wealthy merchant and in 1439–62 president of the judicial bench, was a decided opponent of the Teutonic Knights.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> In 1453 he was the delegate from Toruń at the <a href="/wiki/Grudzi%C4%85dz" title="Grudziądz">Grudziądz</a> (Graudenz) conference that planned the uprising against them.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> During the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Years%27_War_(1454%E2%80%9366)" title="Thirteen Years' War (1454–66)">Thirteen Years' War</a> (1454–66), he actively supported the Prussian cities' war effort with substantial monetary subsidies (only part of which he later re-claimed), with political activity in Toruń and Danzig, and by personally fighting in battles at <a href="/wiki/%C5%81asin" title="Łasin">Łasin</a> (Lessen) and <a href="/wiki/Malbork" title="Malbork">Malbork</a> (Marienburg).<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> He died in 1462.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode_the_Younger" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucas Watzenrode the Younger">Lucas Watzenrode the Younger</a>, the astronomer's maternal uncle and patron, was educated at the University of Kraków (now <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a>) and at the universities of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>. He was a bitter opponent of the Teutonic Order,<sup id="cite_ref-Hirshfeld38_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hirshfeld38-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google12_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google12-23">[22]</a></sup> and its Grand Master once referred to him as "the devil incarnate".<sup id="cite_ref-Moore5262_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore5262-24">[23]</a></sup> In 1489 Watzenrode was elected <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Warmia">Bishop of Warmia</a> (Ermeland, Ermland) against the preference of King Casimir IV, who had hoped to install his own son in that seat.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> As a result, Watzenrode quarreled with the king until Casimir IV's death three years later.<sup id="cite_ref-watzenrode_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watzenrode-26">[25]</a></sup> Watzenrode was then able to form close relations with three successive Polish monarchs: <a href="/wiki/John_I_Albert_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="John I Albert of Poland">John I Albert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Jagiellon" title="Alexander Jagiellon">Alexander Jagiellon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I the Old</a>. He was a friend and key advisor to each ruler, and his influence greatly strengthened the ties between Warmia and Poland proper.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore5262_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore5262-24">[23]</a></sup> Watzenrode came to be considered the most powerful man in Warmia, and his wealth, connections and influence allowed him to secure Copernicus' education and career as a canon at <a href="/wiki/Frombork_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Frombork Cathedral">Frombork Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/170px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png" width="170" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/255px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png/340px-Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png 2x" data-file-width="1599" data-file-height="2515" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-an-Herzog-Albrecht.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/de:An_den_Herzog_Albrecht_von_Preussen" class="extiw" title="wikisource:de:An den Herzog Albrecht von Preussen">German-language letter from Copernicus</a> to <a href="/wiki/Duke_Albert_of_Prussia" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke Albert of Prussia">Duke Albert of Prussia</a>, giving medical advice for <a href="/wiki/George_von_Kunheim" title="George von Kunheim">George von Kunheim</a> (1541)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Copernicus is postulated to have spoken <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> and German with equal fluency. He also spoke <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google16_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google16-30">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google17_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google17-31">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-armitage2-62_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage2-62-32">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[b]</a></sup> The vast majority of Copernicus's surviving works are in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, which in his lifetime was the language of <a href="/wiki/Academia" class="mw-redirect" title="Academia">academia</a> in Europe. Latin was also the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> and of Poland's royal court, and thus all of Copernicus's correspondence with the Church and with Polish leaders was in Latin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>There survive a few documents written by Copernicus in German. The German philosophy professor Martin Carrier mentions this as a reason to consider Copernicus's native language to have been German.<sup id="cite_ref-google18_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google18-37">[35]</a></sup> Other arguments for German being Copernicus's native tongue are that he was born in a predominantly German-speaking city and that, while studying canon law at <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> in 1496, he signed into the German <i><a href="/wiki/Nation_(university)" title="Nation (university)">natio</a></i> (<i>Natio Germanorum</i>)—a student organization which, according to its 1497 by-laws, was open to students of all kingdoms and states whose mother-tongue was German.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen1995_p._127-38">[36]</a></sup> However, according to French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Koyr%C3%A9" title="Alexandre Koyré">Alexandre Koyré</a>, Copernicus's registration with the <i>Natio Germanorum</i> does not in itself imply that Copernicus considered himself German, since students from Prussia and Silesia were routinely so categorized, which carried certain privileges that made it a natural choice for German-speaking students, regardless of their ethnicity or self-identification.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen1995_p._127-38">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google19_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google19-39">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google20_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google20-40">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google21_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google21-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<p>The surname <i>Copernik, Koppernigk</i> is recorded in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> from c. 1350, in various spellings, apparently given to people from the village of <i><a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Köppernigk</a></i>, formerly, prior to 1845 <i><a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Koppirnik</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Copirnik</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Koperniki" title="Koperniki">Copernik</a></i> in Latin, in the Silesian <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Nysa" title="Duchy of Nysa">Duchy of Nysa</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a>, published in 1493, describes of the town of <a href="/wiki/Nysa,_Poland" title="Nysa, Poland">Nysa</a> population as "plebs rustica polonici ydeomatis ...", which may be translated: "rural population idiomatically is Polish ...", or "the vernacular language of the rural population is Polish". Nicolas Copernicus' great-grandfather is recorded as having received citizenship in <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> in 1386. The toponym <i>Köppernig</i> (modern <i>Koperniki</i>) has variously been tied to the German word for <i><a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a></i> (Kupfer) and the Polish word for <i><a href="/wiki/Dill" title="Dill">dill</a></i> (<i>koper</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffix</a> <a href="/wiki/-nik" title="-nik">-nik</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> -niki) denotes a <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> <a href="/wiki/Agent_noun" title="Agent noun">agent noun</a>.</p>
<p>As was common in the period, the spellings of both the <a href="/wiki/Toponym" class="mw-redirect" title="Toponym">toponym</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a> vary greatly. Copernicus "was rather indifferent about <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Gingerich_p143_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich_p143-43">[41]</a></sup> During his childhood, about 1480, the name of his father (and thus of the future astronomer) was recorded in Thorn as <i>Niclas Koppernigk</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-google26_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google26-44">[42]</a></sup> At Kraków he signed himself, in Latin, <i>Nicolaus Nicolai de Torunia</i> (Nicolaus, son of Nicolaus, of Toruń).<sup id="cite_ref-Moore50_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore50-45">[43]</a></sup> At Bologna, in 1496, he registered in the <i>Matricula Nobilissimi Germanorum Collegii, resp. Annales Clarissimae Nacionis Germanorum</i>, of the <i>Natio Germanica Bononiae</i>, as <i>Dominus Nicolaus Kopperlingk de Thorn – IX <a href="/wiki/Groschen" title="Groschen">grosseti</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-google27_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google27-46">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google28_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google28-47">[45]</a></sup> At Padua he signed himself "Nicolaus Copernik", later "Coppernicus".<sup id="cite_ref-Gingerich_p143_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gingerich_p143-43">[41]</a></sup> The astronomer thus Latinized his name to <i>Coppernicus</i>, generally with two "p"s (in 23 of 31 documents studied),<sup id="cite_ref-wikisource_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wikisource-48">[46]</a></sup> but later in life he used a single "p". On the title page of <i>De revolutionibus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Rheticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheticus">Rheticus</a> published the name (in the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Possessive_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive case">possessive</a>, case) as "<i>Nicolai Copernici</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[c]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Poland">In Poland</span></h4>
<p>Upon his father's death, young Nicolaus' maternal uncle, <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode" title="Lucas Watzenrode">Lucas Watzenrode the Younger</a> (1447–1512), took the boy under his wing and saw to his education and career.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Watzenrode maintained contacts with leading intellectual figures in Poland and was a friend of the influential Italian-born <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> <a href="/wiki/Courtier" title="Courtier">courtier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Buonaccorsi" title="Filippo Buonaccorsi">Filippo Buonaccorsi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Polish_Literature_p._38_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polish_Literature_p._38-50">[47]</a></sup> There are no surviving primary documents on the early years of Copernicus's childhood and education.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Copernicus biographers assume that Watzenrode first sent young Copernicus to St. John's School, at Toruń, where he himself had been a master.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Later, according to Armitage,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[d]</a></sup> the boy attended the Cathedral School at <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82oc%C5%82awek" title="Włocławek">Włocławek</a>, up the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vistula River">Vistula River</a> from Toruń, which prepared pupils for entrance to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Krak%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Kraków">University of Kraków</a>, Watzenrode's alma mater in Poland's capital.<sup id="cite_ref-armitage55_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage55-52">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/170px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/255px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG/340px-Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1932" data-file-height="2576" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Krak%C3%B3w_-_Pomnik_Miko%C5%82aja_Kopernika_02.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_Monument_in_Krak%C3%B3w" title="Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Kraków">Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Kraków</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the winter semester of 1491–92 Copernicus, as "Nicolaus Nicolai de Thuronia", matriculated together with his brother Andrew at the University of Kraków (now <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> Copernicus began his studies in the Department of Arts (from the fall of 1491, presumably until the summer or fall of 1495) in the heyday of the <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w_School_of_Mathematics_and_Astrology" title="Kraków School of Mathematics and Astrology">Kraków astronomical-mathematical school</a>, acquiring the foundations for his subsequent mathematical achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup> According to a later but credible tradition (<a href="/wiki/Jan_Bro%C5%BCek" title="Jan Brożek">Jan Brożek</a>), Copernicus was a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Brudzewski" title="Albert Brudzewski">Albert Brudzewski</a>, who by then (from 1491) was a professor of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Aristotelian philosophy">Aristotelian philosophy</a> but taught <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> privately outside the university; Copernicus became familiar with Brudzewski's widely read commentary to <a href="/wiki/Georg_von_Peuerbach" title="Georg von Peuerbach">Georg von Peuerbach</a>'s <i>Theoricæ novæ planetarum</i> and almost certainly attended the lectures of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_of_Biskupie&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bernard of Biskupie (page does not exist)">Bernard of Biskupie</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wojciech_Krypa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wojciech Krypa (page does not exist)">Wojciech Krypa</a> of <a href="/wiki/Szamotu%C5%82y" title="Szamotuły">Szamotuły</a>, and probably other astronomical lectures by <a href="/wiki/Jan_of_G%C5%82og%C3%B3w" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan of Głogów">Jan of Głogów</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micha%C5%82_of_Wroc%C5%82aw&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Michał of Wrocław (page does not exist)">Michał of Wrocław</a> (Breslau), <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wojciech_of_Pniewy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wojciech of Pniewy (page does not exist)">Wojciech of Pniewy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marcin_Bylica" title="Marcin Bylica">Marcin Bylica</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olkusz" title="Olkusz">Olkusz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4-5_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-5-53">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus' Kraków studies gave him a thorough grounding in the mathematical astronomy taught at the University (arithmetic, geometry, geometric optics, cosmography, theoretical and computational astronomy) and a good knowledge of the philosophical and natural-science writings of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/De_coelo" class="mw-redirect" title="De coelo">De coelo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (which in the future would play an important role in the shaping of Copernicus' theory), stimulating his interest in learning and making him conversant with <a href="/wiki/Humanistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Humanistic">humanistic</a> culture.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup> Copernicus broadened the knowledge that he took from the university lecture halls with independent reading of books that he acquired during his Kraków years (<a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haly_Abenragel" title="Haly Abenragel">Haly Abenragel</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Alfonsine_Tables" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfonsine Tables">Alfonsine Tables</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Regiomontanus" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes Regiomontanus">Johannes Regiomontanus</a>' <i>Tabulae directionum</i>); to this period, probably, also date his earliest scientific notes, now preserved partly at <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University" title="Uppsala University">Uppsala University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup> At Kraków Copernicus began collecting a large library on astronomy; it would later be carried off as war booty by the Swedes during the <a href="/wiki/Deluge_(history)" title="Deluge (history)">Deluge</a> in the 1650s and is now at the <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University_Library" title="Uppsala University Library">Uppsala University Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus' four years at Kraków played an important role in the development of his critical faculties and initiated his analysis of logical contradictions in the two "official" systems of astronomy—Aristotle's theory of homocentric spheres, and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s mechanism of <a href="/wiki/Deferent" class="mw-redirect" title="Deferent">eccentrics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Epicycle">epicycles</a>—the surmounting and discarding of which would be the first step toward the creation of Copernicus' own doctrine of the structure of the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Without taking a degree, probably in the fall of 1495, Copernicus left Kraków for the court of his uncle Watzenrode, who in 1489 had been elevated to <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Bishop of Warmia">Prince-Bishop of Warmia</a> and soon (before November 1495) sought to place his nephew in the Warmia <a href="/wiki/Canonry" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonry">canonry</a> vacated by the 26 August 1495 death of its previous tenant, Jan Czanow. For unclear reasons—probably due to opposition from part of the chapter, who appealed to Rome—Copernicus' installation was delayed, inclining Watzenrode to send both his nephews to study canon law in Italy, seemingly with a view to furthering their ecclesiastic careers and thereby also strengthening his own influence in the Warmia chapter.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leaving Warmia in mid-1496—possibly with the retinue of the chapter's chancellor, Jerzy Pranghe, who was going to Italy—in the fall, possibly in October, Copernicus arrived in <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> and a few months later (after 6 January 1497) signed himself into the register of the Bologna University of Jurists' "German nation", which included young Poles from <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a> as well as students of other nationalities.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Italy">In Italy</span></h4>
<p>It was only on 20 October 1497 that Copernicus, by proxy, formally succeeded to the Warmia canonry which had been granted to him two years earlier. To this, by a document dated 10 January 1503 at <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, he would add a <a href="/wiki/Sinecure" title="Sinecure">sinecure</a> at the Collegiate Church of the Holy Cross in <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>. Despite having been granted a papal <a href="/wiki/Indult" title="Indult">indult</a> on 29 November 1508 to receive further <a href="/wiki/Benefice" title="Benefice">benefices</a>, through his ecclesiastic career Copernicus not only did not acquire further <a href="/wiki/Prebend" class="mw-redirect" title="Prebend">prebends</a> and higher stations (<a href="/wiki/Prelacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Prelacy">prelacies</a>) at the chapter, but in 1538 he relinquished the Wrocław sinecure. It is unclear whether he was ever ordained a priest.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Rosen" title="Edward Rosen">Edward Rosen</a> asserts that he was not.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> Copernicus did take <a href="/wiki/Minor_orders" title="Minor orders">minor orders</a>, which sufficed for assuming a chapter canonry.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-27">[26]</a></sup> <i>The Catholic Encyclopedia</i> proposes that his ordination was probable, as in 1537 he was one of four candidates for the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">episcopal seat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Warmia" title="Warmia">Warmia</a>, a position which required ordination.<sup id="cite_ref-newadvent.org_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newadvent.org-58">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg/170px-Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg/255px-Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg/340px-Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Domenico_Maria_Novara_house_location.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Via Galliera 65, <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, site of house of <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Maria_Novara_da_Ferrara" title="Domenico Maria Novara da Ferrara">Domenico Maria Novara</a>. <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_plaque" title="Commemorative plaque">Plaque</a> on <a href="/wiki/Portico" title="Portico">portico</a> commemorates Copernicus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his three-year stay at Bologna, between fall 1496 and spring 1501, Copernicus seems to have devoted himself less keenly to studying <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> (he received his doctorate in law only after seven years, following a second return to Italy in 1503) than to studying the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>—probably attending lectures by <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Beroaldo" title="Filippo Beroaldo">Filippo Beroaldo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Urceo" title="Antonio Urceo">Antonio Urceo</a>, called Codro, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Garzoni" title="Giovanni Garzoni">Giovanni Garzoni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Achillini" title="Alessandro Achillini">Alessandro Achillini</a>—and to studying astronomy. He met the famous astronomer <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Maria_Novara_da_Ferrara" title="Domenico Maria Novara da Ferrara">Domenico Maria Novara da Ferrara</a> and became his disciple and assistant.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> Copernicus was developing new ideas inspired by reading the "Epitome of the Almagest" (<i>Epitome in Almagestum Ptolemei</i>) by <a href="/wiki/George_von_Peuerbach" class="mw-redirect" title="George von Peuerbach">George von Peuerbach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Regiomontanus" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes Regiomontanus">Johannes Regiomontanus</a> (Venice, 1496). He verified its observations about certain peculiarities in Ptolemy's theory of the Moon's motion, by conducting on 9 March 1497 at Bologna a memorable observation of the <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aldebaran" title="Aldebaran">Aldebaran</a>, the brightest star in the <a href="/wiki/Taurus_(constellation)" title="Taurus (constellation)">Taurus</a> constellation, by the moon. Copernicus the humanist sought confirmation for his growing doubts through close reading of Greek and Latin authors (<a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristarchos_of_Samos" class="mw-redirect" title="Aristarchos of Samos">Aristarchos of Samos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleomedes" title="Cleomedes">Cleomedes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philolaus" title="Philolaus">Philolaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heraclides_Ponticus" title="Heraclides Ponticus">Heraclides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecphantos" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecphantos">Ecphantos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>), gathering, especially while at <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, fragmentary historic information about ancient astronomical, <a href="/wiki/Cosmological" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmological">cosmological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a> systems.<sup id="cite_ref-psb5-6_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb5-6-60">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg/250px-Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg" width="250" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg/375px-Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/36/Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg/500px-Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1468" data-file-height="901" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernico_commemorative_plate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Here, where stood the house of <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Maria_Novara_da_Ferrara" title="Domenico Maria Novara da Ferrara">Domenico Maria Novara</a>, professor of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Studium" class="mw-redirect" title="Studium">Studium</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, NICOLAUS COPERNICUS, the Polish mathematician and astronomer who would revolutionize concepts of the universe, conducted brilliant celestial observations with his teacher in 1497–1500. Placed on the 5th centenary of [Copernicus's] birth by the <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">City</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University</a>, the Academy of Sciences of the Institute of Bologna, the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Polish Academy of Sciences">Polish Academy of Sciences</a>. 1473 [—] 1973."</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Copernicus spent the <a href="/wiki/Jubilee_(Christianity)" title="Jubilee (Christianity)">jubilee year</a> 1500 in Rome, where he arrived with his brother Andrew that spring, doubtless to perform an apprenticeship at the <a href="/wiki/Papal_Curia" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal Curia">Papal Curia</a>. Here, too, however, he continued his astronomical work begun at Bologna, observing, for example, a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a> on the night of 5–6 November 1500. According to a later account by <a href="/wiki/Rheticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheticus">Rheticus</a>, Copernicus also—probably privately, rather than at the Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Sapienza" class="mw-redirect" title="Sapienza">Sapienza</a></i>—as a "<i>Professor Mathematum</i>" (professor of astronomy) delivered, "to numerous... students and... leading masters of the science", public lectures devoted probably to a critique of the mathematical solutions of contemporary astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>On his return journey doubtless stopping briefly at Bologna, in mid-1501 Copernicus arrived back in Warmia. After on 28 July receiving from the chapter a two-year extension of leave in order to study medicine (since "he may in future be a useful medical advisor to our Reverend Superior [Bishop <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Watzenrode" title="Lucas Watzenrode">Lucas Watzenrode</a>] and the gentlemen of the chapter"), in late summer or in the fall he returned again to Italy, probably accompanied by his brother Andrew<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> and by Canon Bernhard Sculteti. This time he studied at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a>, famous as a seat of medical learning, and—except for a brief visit to <a href="/wiki/Ferrara" title="Ferrara">Ferrara</a> in May–June 1503 to pass examinations for, and receive, his doctorate in canon law—he remained at Padua from fall 1501 to summer 1503.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus studied medicine probably under the direction of leading Padua professors—Bartolomeo da Montagnana, <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Fracastoro" title="Girolamo Fracastoro">Girolamo Fracastoro</a>, Gabriele Zerbi, Alessandro Benedetti—and read medical treatises that he acquired at this time, by Valescus de Taranta, Jan Mesue, Hugo Senensis, Jan Ketham, Arnold de Villa Nova, and Michele Savonarola, which would form the embryo of his later medical library.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the subjects that Copernicus must have studied was <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, since it was considered an important part of a medical education.<sup id="cite_ref-reference_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reference-63">[59]</a></sup> However, unlike most other prominent Renaissance astronomers, he appears never to have practiced or expressed any interest in astrology.<sup id="cite_ref-countenanced_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countenanced-64">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>As at Bologna, Copernicus did not limit himself to his official studies. It was probably the Padua years that saw the beginning of his Hellenistic interests. He familiarized himself with Greek language and culture with the aid of <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_Gaza" title="Theodorus Gaza">Theodorus Gaza</a>'s grammar (1495) and J.B. Chrestonius' dictionary (1499), expanding his studies of antiquity, begun at Bologna, to the writings of <a href="/wiki/Basilius_Bessarion" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilius Bessarion">Basilius Bessarion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Valla" title="Lorenzo Valla">Lorenzo Valla</a> and others. There also seems to be evidence that it was during his Padua stay that the idea finally crystallized, of basing a new system of the world on the movement of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup> As the time approached for Copernicus to return home, in spring 1503 he journeyed to Ferrara where, on 31 May 1503, having passed the obligatory examinations, he was granted the degree of doctor of canon law (<i>Nicolaus Copernich de Prusia, Jure Canonico ... et doctoratus</i><sup id="cite_ref-NCGes_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCGes-65">[61]</a></sup>). No doubt it was soon after (at latest, in fall 1503) that he left Italy for good to return to <a href="/wiki/Warmia" title="Warmia">Warmia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_observations">Planetary observations</span></h3>
<p>Copernicus made three observations of Mercury, with errors of -3, -15 and -1 minutes of arc. He made one of Venus, with an error of -24 minutes. Four were made of Mars, with errors of 2, 20, 77, and 137 minutes. Four observations were made of Jupiter, with errors of 32, 51, -11 and 25 minutes. He made four of Saturn, with errors of 31, 20, 23 and -4 minutes. <sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work">Work</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg/220px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg/330px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg/440px-Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="558" data-file-height="398" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolaus_Kopernikus_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Copernicus,_or_Conversations_with_God" title="Astronomer Copernicus, or Conversations with God">Astronomer Copernicus, or Conversations with God</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko" title="Jan Matejko">Matejko</a>. In background: <a href="/wiki/Frombork_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Frombork Cathedral">Frombork Cathedral</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Having completed all his studies in Italy, 30-year-old Copernicus returned to Warmia, where he would live out the remaining 40 years of his life, apart from brief journeys to <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> and to nearby Prussian cities: <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Toruń</a> (Thorn), <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> (Danzig), <a href="/wiki/Elbl%C4%85g" title="Elbląg">Elbląg</a> (Elbing), <a href="/wiki/Grudzi%C4%85dz" title="Grudziądz">Grudziądz</a> (Graudenz), <a href="/wiki/Malbork" title="Malbork">Malbork</a> (Marienburg), <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a> (Królewiec).<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishopric_of_Warmia" title="Prince-Bishopric of Warmia">Prince-Bishopric of Warmia</a> enjoyed substantial <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>, with its own <a href="/wiki/Diet_(assembly)" title="Diet (assembly)">diet</a> (parliament) and monetary unit (the same as in the other parts of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussia</a>) and treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-sedlar_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sedlar-67">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus was his uncle's secretary and physician from 1503 to 1510 (or perhaps till his uncle's death on 29 March 1512) and resided in the Bishop's castle at <a href="/wiki/Lidzbark_Warmi%C5%84ski" title="Lidzbark Warmiński">Lidzbark</a> (Heilsberg), where he began work on his heliocentric theory. In his official capacity, he took part in nearly all his uncle's political, ecclesiastic and administrative-economic duties. From the beginning of 1504, Copernicus accompanied Watzenrode to sessions of the Royal Prussian diet held at Malbork and Elbląg and, write Dobrzycki and Hajdukiewicz, "participated... in all the more important events in the complex diplomatic game that ambitious politician and statesman played in defense of the particular interests of Prussia and Warmia, between hostility to the [Teutonic] Order and loyalty to the Polish Crown."<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg/170px-Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg/255px-Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg/340px-Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="585" data-file-height="811" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Symokatta_epistole_morales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copernicus's translation of <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a>'s <i>Epistles</i>. Cover shows <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coats-of-arms</a> of <i>(clockwise from top)</i> <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, Lithuania and <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1504–12 Copernicus made numerous journeys as part of his uncle's retinue—in 1504, to Toruń and <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a>, to a session of the Royal Prussian Council in the presence of Poland's King <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Jagiellon" title="Alexander Jagiellon">Alexander Jagiellon</a>; to sessions of the Prussian diet at Malbork (1506), Elbląg (1507) and <a href="/wiki/Sztum" title="Sztum">Sztum</a> (Stuhm) (1512); and he may have attended a <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a> (Posen) session (1510) and the coronation of Poland's King <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I the Old</a> in Kraków (1507). Watzenrode's itinerary suggests that in spring 1509 Copernicus may have attended the <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a> <a href="/wiki/Sejm" title="Sejm">sejm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb6_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb6-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was probably on the latter occasion, in Kraków, that Copernicus submitted for printing at <a href="/wiki/Jan_Haller" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan Haller">Jan Haller</a>'s press his translation, from Greek to Latin, of a collection, by the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a>, of 85 brief poems called Epistles, or letters, supposed to have passed between various characters in a Greek story. They are of three kinds—"moral," offering advice on how people should live; "pastoral", giving little pictures of shepherd life; and "amorous", comprising love poems. They are arranged to follow one another in a regular rotation of subjects. Copernicus had translated the Greek verses into Latin prose, and he now published his version as <i>Theophilacti scolastici Simocati epistolae morales, rurales et amatoriae interpretatione latina</i>, which he dedicated to his uncle in gratitude for all the benefits he had received from him. With this translation, Copernicus declared himself on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanists</a> in the struggle over the question whether Greek literature should be revived.<sup id="cite_ref-armitage75.E2.80.9377_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage75.E2.80.9377-33">[32]</a></sup> Copernicus's first poetic work was a Greek <a href="/wiki/Epigram" title="Epigram">epigram</a>, composed probably during a visit to Kraków, for <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Dantiscus" title="Johannes Dantiscus">Johannes Dantiscus</a>' <a href="/wiki/Epithalamium" title="Epithalamium">epithalamium</a> for <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Zapolya" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbara Zapolya">Barbara Zapolya</a>'s 1512 wedding to <a href="/wiki/Monarchs_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Monarchs of Poland">King</a> <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Zygmunt I the Old</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some time before 1514, Copernicus wrote an initial outline of his heliocentric theory known only from later transcripts, by the title (perhaps given to it by a copyist), <i>Nicolai Copernici de hypothesibus motuum coelestium a se constitutis commentariolus</i>—commonly referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Commentariolus" title="Commentariolus">Commentariolus</a></i>. It was a succinct theoretical description of the world's heliocentric mechanism, without mathematical apparatus, and differed in some important details of geometric construction from <i>De revolutionibus</i>; but it was already based on the same assumptions regarding Earth's triple motions. The <i>Commentariolus</i>, which Copernicus consciously saw as merely a first sketch for his planned book, was not intended for printed distribution. He made only a very few manuscript copies available to his closest acquaintances, including, it seems, several Kraków astronomers with whom he collaborated in 1515–30 in observing <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">eclipses</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> would include a fragment from the <i><a href="/wiki/Commentariolus" title="Commentariolus">Commentariolus</a></i> in his own treatise, <i>Astronomiae instauratae progymnasmata</i>, published in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> in 1602, based on a manuscript that he had received from the <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemian</a> physician and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Tade%C3%A1%C5%A1_H%C3%A1jek" title="Tadeáš Hájek">Tadeáš Hájek</a>, a friend of <a href="/wiki/Rheticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Rheticus">Rheticus</a>. The <i>Commentariolus</i> would appear complete in print for the first time only in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg/170px-Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg/255px-Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg/340px-Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus_Tower_in_Frombork.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Copernicus' tower at <a href="/wiki/Frombork" title="Frombork">Frombork</a>, where he lived and worked; reconstructed since World War II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1510 or 1512 Copernicus moved to Frombork, a town to the northwest at the <a href="/wiki/Vistula_Lagoon" title="Vistula Lagoon">Vistula Lagoon</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> coast. There, in April 1512, he participated in the election of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Fabian_of_Lossainen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Fabian of Lossainen (page does not exist)">Fabian of Lossainen</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Bishop of Warmia">Prince-Bishop of Warmia</a>. It was only in early June 1512 that the chapter gave Copernicus an "external curia"—a house outside the defensive walls of the cathedral mount. In 1514 he purchased the northwestern tower within the walls of the Frombork stronghold. He would maintain both these residences to the end of his life, despite the devastation of the chapter's buildings by a raid against Frauenburg carried out by the Teutonic Order in January 1520, during which Copernicus's astronomical instruments were probably destroyed. Copernicus conducted astronomical observations in 1513–16 presumably from his external curia; and in 1522–43, from an unidentified "small tower" (<i>turricula</i>), using primitive instruments modeled on ancient ones—the <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">quadrant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triquetrum_(astronomy)" title="Triquetrum (astronomy)">triquetrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a>. At Frombork Copernicus conducted over half of his more than 60 registered astronomical observations.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Having settled permanently at Frombork, where he would reside to the end of his life, with interruptions in 1516–19 and 1520–21, Copernicus found himself at the Warmia chapter's economic and administrative center, which was also one of Warmia's two chief centers of political life. In the difficult, politically complex situation of Warmia, threatened externally by the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a>'s aggressions (attacks by Teutonic bands; the <a href="/wiki/Polish-Teutonic_War_(1519-1521)" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish-Teutonic War (1519-1521)">Polish-Teutonic War of 1519–21</a>; Albert's plans to annex Warmia), internally subject to strong separatist pressures (the selection of the <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Bishop of Warmia">prince-bishops of Warmia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Currency_reform" class="mw-redirect" title="Currency reform">currency reform</a>), he, together with part of the chapter, represented a program of strict cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Crown">Polish Crown</a> and demonstrated in all his public activities (the defense of his country against the Order's plans of conquest; proposals to unify its <a href="/wiki/Monetary" class="mw-redirect" title="Monetary">monetary</a> system with the Polish Crown's; support for Poland's interests in the Warmia dominion's ecclesiastic administration) that he was consciously a citizen of the <a href="/wiki/Polish-Lithuanian_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish-Lithuanian Republic">Polish-Lithuanian Republic</a>. Soon after the death of uncle Bishop Watzenrode, he participated in the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Treaty_of_Piotrk%C3%B3w_Trybunalski" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Treaty of Piotrków Trybunalski">Second Treaty of Piotrków Trybunalski</a> (7 December 1512), governing the appointment of the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Warmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Warmia">Bishop of Warmia</a>, declaring, despite opposition from part of the chapter, for loyal cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Crown">Polish Crown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/220px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/330px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG/440px-Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1932" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frombork_-_Wzg%C3%B3rze_katedralne.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Frombork_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Frombork Cathedral">Frombork Cathedral</a> mount and fortifications. In foreground: statue of Copernicus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>That same year (before 8 November 1512) Copernicus assumed responsibility, as <i>magister pistoriae</i>, for administering the chapter's economic enterprises (he would hold this office again in 1530), having already since 1511 fulfilled the duties of chancellor and visitor of the chapter's estates.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-68">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>His administrative and economic dutes did not distract Copernicus, in 1512–15, from intensive observational activity. The results of his observations of <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> in this period, and especially a series of four observations of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> made in 1515, led to discovery of the variability of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> and of the movement of the solar <a href="/wiki/Apogee" class="mw-redirect" title="Apogee">apogee</a> in relation to the <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed stars</a>, which in 1515–19 prompted his first revisions of certain assumptions of his system. Some of the observations that he made in this period may have had a connection with a proposed reform of the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> made in the first half of 1513 at the request of the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Fossombrone" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Fossombrone">Bishop of Fossombrone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Middelburg" title="Paul of Middelburg">Paul of Middelburg</a>. Their contacts in this matter in the period of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Lateran_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifth Lateran Council">Fifth Lateran Council</a> were later memorialized in a complimentary mention in Copernicus's dedicatory epistle in <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">Dē revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> and in a treatise by Paul of Middelburg, <i>Secundum compendium correctionis Calendarii</i> (1516), which mentions Copernicus among the learned men who had sent the Council proposals for the calendar's emendation.<sup id="cite_ref-psb7-8_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb7-8-69">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Olsztyn-zamek.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olsztyn-zamek.jpg/220px-Olsztyn-zamek.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olsztyn-zamek.jpg/330px-Olsztyn-zamek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olsztyn-zamek.jpg/440px-Olsztyn-zamek.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="481" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Olsztyn-zamek.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Olsztyn" title="Olsztyn">Olsztyn Castle</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During 1516–21, Copernicus resided at Olsztyn (Allenstein) Castle as economic administrator of Warmia, including <a href="/wiki/Olsztyn" title="Olsztyn">Olsztyn</a> (Allenstein) and <a href="/wiki/Pieni%C4%99%C5%BCno" title="Pieniężno">Pieniężno</a> (Mehlsack). While there, he wrote a manuscript, <i><a href="/wiki/Locationes_mansorum_desertorum" title="Locationes mansorum desertorum">Locationes mansorum desertorum</a></i> (<i>Locations of Deserted Fiefs</i>), with a view to populating those fiefs with industrious farmers and so bolstering the economy of Warmia. When <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Olsztyn" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Olsztyn">Olsztyn was besieged</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Teutonic_War_(1519%E2%80%9321)" title="Polish–Teutonic War (1519–21)">Polish–Teutonic War</a>, Copernicus directed the defense of Olsztyn and Warmia by Royal Polish forces. He also represented the Polish side in the ensuing peace negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-repcheck66_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-repcheck66-70">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Copernicus for years advised the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prussia" title="Royal Prussia">Royal Prussian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sejmik" title="Sejmik">sejmik</a> on <a href="/wiki/Monetary_reform" title="Monetary reform">monetary reform</a>, particularly in the 1520s when that was a major question in regional Prussian politics.<sup id="cite_ref-psb9_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb9-71">[67]</a></sup> In 1526 he wrote a study on the value of money, <i><a href="/wiki/Monetae_cudendae_ratio" title="Monetae cudendae ratio">Monetae cudendae ratio</a></i>. In it he formulated an early iteration of the theory, now called <a href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_law" title="Gresham's law">Gresham's law</a>, that "bad" (<a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">debased</a>) <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a> drives "good" (un-debased) coinage out of circulation—several decades before <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gresham" title="Thomas Gresham">Thomas Gresham</a>. He also, in 1517, set down a <a href="/wiki/Quantity_theory_of_money" title="Quantity theory of money">quantity theory of money</a>, a principal concept in economics to the present day. Copernicus's recommendations on monetary reform were widely read by leaders of both Prussia and Poland in their attempts to stabilize currency.<sup id="cite_ref-beginnings_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beginnings-72">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:6_Warszawa_153.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/6_Warszawa_153.jpg/170px-6_Warszawa_153.jpg" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/6_Warszawa_153.jpg/255px-6_Warszawa_153.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/6_Warszawa_153.jpg/340px-6_Warszawa_153.jpg 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="750" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:6_Warszawa_153.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_Monument_in_Warsaw" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicolaus Copernicus Monument in Warsaw">Copernicus Monument in Warsaw</a> designed by the Danish sculptor <a href="/wiki/Bertel_Thorvaldsen" title="Bertel Thorvaldsen">Bertel Thorvaldsen</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1533, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Albrecht_Widmannstetter" title="Johann Albrecht Widmannstetter">Johann Widmanstetter</a>, secretary to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a>, explained Copernicus's heliocentric system to the Pope and two cardinals. The Pope was so pleased that he gave Widmanstetter a valuable gift.<sup id="cite_ref-Repcheck_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-73">[69]</a></sup> In 1535 <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Wapowski" title="Bernard Wapowski">Bernard Wapowski</a> wrote a letter to a gentleman in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, urging him to publish an enclosed <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">almanac</a>, which he claimed had been written by Copernicus. This is the only mention of a Copernicus almanac in the historical records. The "almanac" was likely Copernicus's tables of planetary positions. Wapowski's letter mentions Copernicus's theory about the motions of the earth. Nothing came of Wapowski's request, because he died a couple of weeks later.<sup id="cite_ref-Repcheck_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-73">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the death of Prince-Bishop of Warmia <a href="/wiki/Mauritius_Ferber" title="Mauritius Ferber">Mauritius Ferber</a> (1 July 1537), Copernicus participated in the election of his successor, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Dantiscus" title="Johannes Dantiscus">Johannes Dantiscus</a> (20 September 1537). Copernicus was one of four candidates for the post, written in at the initiative of <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a>; but his candidacy was actually <i><a href="/wiki/Pro_forma" title="Pro forma">pro forma</a></i>, since Dantiscus had earlier been named <a href="/wiki/Coadjutor_bishop" title="Coadjutor bishop">coadjutor bishop</a> to Ferber and since Dantiscus had the backing of Poland's King <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_I_the_Old" title="Sigismund I the Old">Sigismund I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-psb11_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb11-74">[70]</a></sup> At first Copernicus maintained friendly relations with the new Prince-Bishop, assisting him medically in spring 1538 and accompanying him that summer on an inspection tour of Chapter holdings. But that autumn, their friendship was strained by suspicions over Copernicus's housekeeper, Anna Schilling, whom Dantiscus banished from Frombork in spring 1539.<sup id="cite_ref-psb11_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb11-74">[70]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kopernikus,_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg/170px-Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg/255px-Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg/340px-Kopernikus%2C_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1224" data-file-height="1624" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kopernikus,_Nikolaus_-_Reu%C3%9Fner_1578_Portrait1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of Copernicus holding a <a href="/wiki/Lily_of_the_valley" title="Lily of the valley">lily of the valley</a>, published in <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Reusner" title="Nicolaus Reusner">Nicolaus Reusner</a>'s <i>Icones</i> (1587), based on a sketch by <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Stimmer" title="Tobias Stimmer">Tobias Stimmer</a> (c. 1570), allegedly based on a self-portrait by Copernicus. This portrait became the basis of most later depictions of Copernicus.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his younger days, Copernicus the physician had treated his uncle, brother and other chapter members. In later years he was called upon to attend the elderly bishops who in turn occupied the see of Warmia—Mauritius Ferber and Johannes Dantiscus – and, in 1539, his old friend <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a>, Bishop of <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno" title="Chełmno">Chełmno</a> (Kulm). In treating such important patients, he sometimes sought consultations from other physicians, including the physician to Duke Albert and, by letter, the Polish Royal Physician.<sup id="cite_ref-armitage97.E2.80.9398_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage97.E2.80.9398-76">[72]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-Boissard.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Copernicus-Boissard.gif/170px-Copernicus-Boissard.gif" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Copernicus-Boissard.gif/255px-Copernicus-Boissard.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Copernicus-Boissard.gif/340px-Copernicus-Boissard.gif 2x" data-file-width="411" data-file-height="528" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copernicus-Boissard.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"<i>Nicolaus Copernicus <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toruń">Tornaeus</a> <a href="/wiki/Prussia_(region)" title="Prussia (region)">Borussus</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">Mathemat.</a></i>", 1597</div>
</div>
</div>
