<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Linton-2004">Linton 2004</a>, pp.&#160;39, 119. The Greek mathematician and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> proposed such a system during the third century BCE, but there is little evidence that his ideas were developed beyond a basic outline (<a href="#Reference-Dreyer-1953">Dreyer 1953</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/historyofplaneta00dreyuoft#page/134/mode/2up">pp.&#160;135–48</a>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Nicolaus_Copernicus#Dubious" title="Talk:Nicolaus Copernicus">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>. Copernicus was aware of Aristarchus' heliocentric theory and cited him in an early (unpublished) manuscript of <i>De Revolutionibus</i> (which still survives), though he removed the reference from his final published manuscript.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He used <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and German, knew enough <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> to translate the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Theophylact_Simocatta" title="Theophylact Simocatta">Theophylact Simocatta</a>'s verses into Latin prose<sup id="cite_ref-armitage75.E2.80.9377_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-armitage75.E2.80.9377-33">[32]</a></sup> and "there is ample evidence that he knew the <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish language</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Davies26_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies26-34">[33]</a></sup> During his several years' studies in Italy, Copernicus presumably would also have learned some Italian. Professor Stefan Melkowski of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus_University_in_Toru%C5%84" title="Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń">Nicolaus Copernicus University in Toruń</a> likewise asserts that Copernicus spoke both German and Polish.<sup id="cite_ref-O_historii_i_o_wsp.C3.B3.C5.82czesno.C5.9Bci_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_historii_i_o_wsp.C3.B3.C5.82czesno.C5.9Bci-35">[34]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See the title page at <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus#The_book" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">The book</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dobrzycki and Hajdukiewicz (1969) describe Copernicus having attended school at <a href="/wiki/W%C5%82oc%C5%82awek" title="Włocławek">Włocławek</a> as unlikely.<sup id="cite_ref-psb4_13-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psb4-13">[12]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A reference to the "Commentariolus" is contained in a library catalogue, dated 1 May 1514, of a 16th-century historian, Matthew of <a href="/wiki/Miech%C3%B3w" title="Miechów">Miechów</a>, so it must have begun circulating before that date (Koyré, <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">1973, p. 85</a>; Gingerich, <a href="#Reference-Gingerich-2004">2004, p. 32</a>). <a href="#thoren-1990">Thoren</a> (1990 p. 99) gives the length of the manuscript as 40 pages.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Koyré <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">(1973, pp. 27, 90)</a> and Rosen <a href="#Reference-Rosen-1995">(1995, pp. 64,184)</a> take the view that Copernicus was indeed concerned about possible objections from theologians, while Lindberg and Numbers <a href="#Reference-Lindberg.26Numbers-1986">(1986)</a> argue against it. Koestler <a href="#Reference-Koestler-1963">(1963)</a> also denies it. Indirect evidence that Copernicus was concerned about objections from theologians comes from a letter written to him by <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Osiander" title="Andreas Osiander">Andreas Osiander</a> in 1541, in which Osiander advises Copernicus to adopt a proposal by which he says "you will be able to appease the Peripatetics and theologians whose opposition you fear". <a href="#Reference-Koyre-1973">(Koyré, 1973, pp. 35, 90)</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Particularly his use of the <a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a> and his models for the motions of Mercury and the Moon (Linton <a href="#Reference-Linton-2004">2004</a>, pp. 124, 137–38)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rosen <a href="#Reference-Rosen-1995">(1995, pp. 187–92)</a>, originally published in 1967 in <i>Saggi su Galileo Galilei</i> . Rosen is particularly scathing about this and other statements in <i>The Sleepwalkers</i>, which he criticizes as inaccurate.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fantoli <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(2005, pp. 118–19)</a>; Finocchiaro <a href="#Reference-Finocchiaro-1989">(1989, pp. 148, 153)</a>. On-line copies of Finocchiaro's translations of the relevant documents, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#inqminutes">Inquisition Minutes of 25 February 1616</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#certificate">Cardinal Bellarmine's certificate of 26 May 1616</a>, have been made available by Gagné <a href="#Reference-Gagne-2005">(2005)</a>. This notice of the decree would not have prevented Galileo from discussing heliocentrism solely as a mathematical hypothesis, but a stronger <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html#specinj">formal injunction</a> <a href="#Reference-Finocchiaro-1989">(Finocchiaro, 1989, p. 147-148)</a> not to teach it "in any way whatever, either orally or in writing", allegedly issued to him by the Commissary of the Holy Office, Father Michelangelo Segizzi, would certainly have done so <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(Fantoli, 2005, pp. 119–20, 137)</a>. There has been much controversy over whether the copy of this injunction in the Vatican archives is authentic; if so, whether it was ever issued; and if so, whether it was legally valid <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(Fantoli, 2005, pp. 120–43)</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The oldest known portrait of Copernicus is that on <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_astronomical_clock" title="Strasbourg astronomical clock">Strasbourg astronomical clock</a>, made by <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Stimmer" title="Tobias Stimmer">Tobias Stimmer</a> c. 1571–4. According to the inscription next to the portrait, it was made from a self-portrait by Copernicus himself. This has led to speculation that the Torun portrait may be a copy based on the same self-portrait, but its provenance is unknown. André Goddu, <i>Copernicus and the Aristotelian Tradition</i> (2010), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=iEjk13-1xSYC&amp;pg=PA436">p. 436</a> (note 125), citing Goddu, review of: Jerzy Gassowski,<i>Poszukiwanie grobu Mikolaja Kopernika</i> in: <i>Journal for the History of Astronomy</i> 38.2 (May 2007), p. 255.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFJones2003" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">Jones, Daniel</a> (2003) [1917], Peter Roach, James Hartmann and Jane Setter, eds., <i>English Pronouncing Dictionary</i>, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3-12-539683-2" title="Special:BookSources/3-12-539683-2">3-12-539683-2</a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANicolaus+Copernicus&amp;rft.aufirst=Daniel&amp;rft.aulast=Jones&amp;rft.btitle=English+Pronouncing+Dictionary&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=3-12-539683-2&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span><span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33; margin-left:0.3em">CS1 maint: Uses editors parameter (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Uses_editors_parameter" title="Category:CS1 maint: Uses editors parameter">link</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dictionary.com/browse/Copernicus">"Copernicus"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary.com" title="Dictionary.com">Dictionary.com</a> Unabridged</i>. <a href="/wiki/Random_House" title="Random House">Random House</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANicolaus+Copernicus&amp;rft.atitle=Copernicus&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Dictionary.com+Unabridged&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.dictionary.com%2Fbrowse%2FCopernicus&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">modern pronunciation of the Polish form of the name: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[miˈkɔwaj kɔˈpɛrɲik]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Pl-Miko%C5%82aj_Kopernik.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Pl-Miko%C5%82aj_Kopernik.ogg" class="internal" title="Pl-Mikołaj Kopernik.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Owen_Gingerich" title="Owen Gingerich">Owen Gingerich</a>, "Did Copernicus Owe a Debt to Aristarchus?", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_for_the_History_of_Astronomy" title="Journal for the History of Astronomy">Journal for the History of Astronomy</a></i>, vol. 16, no. 1 (February 1985), pp. 37–42.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Dava_Sobel" title="Dava Sobel">Dava Sobel</a> writes: "Copernicus had no idea that Aristarchus of Samos had proposed much the same thing [as Copernicus was contemplating by 1510, when he wrote his <i>Brief Sketch</i>, otherwise also known as the <i>Commentariolus</i>] in the third century B.C. The only work by Aristarchus known to Copernicus—a treatise called <i>On the Sizes and Distances of the Sun and Moon</i>—made no mention of a heliocentric plan." Sobel (2011) pp. 18–19. Sobel further writes that in Copernicus' dedication of <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Revolutions_of_the_Heavenly_Spheres" class="mw-redirect" title="On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres">On the Revolutions</a></i> to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>—which Copernicus hoped would dampen criticism of his <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> by "babblers... completely ignorant of [astronomy]"—the book's author wrote that, in rereading all of philosophy, in the pages of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> he had found references to those few thinkers who dared to move the Earth "against the traditional opinion of astronomers and almost against common sense." Sobel comments: "He still knew nothing of the Earth-moving plan of Aristarchus, which had not yet been reported to Latin audiences." (pp. 179–82).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Edward_Rosen" title="Edward Rosen">Edward Rosen</a>, "Copernicus, Nicolaus", <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Americana" title="Encyclopedia Americana">Encyclopedia Americana</a></i>, International Edition, volume 7, Danbury, Connecticut, Grolier Incorporated, 1986, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7172-0117-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-7172-0117-1">0-7172-0117-1</a>, pp. 755–56.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-armitage91-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-armitage91_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Copernicus seems to have drawn up some notes [on the displacement of good coin from circulation by debased coin] while he was at <a href="/wiki/Olsztyn" title="Olsztyn">Olsztyn</a> in 1519. He made them the basis of a report on the matter, written in German, which he presented to the Prussian Diet held in 1522 at <a href="/wiki/Grudzi%C4%85dz" title="Grudziądz">Grudziądz</a>... He later drew up a revised and enlarged version of his little treatise, this time in Latin, and setting forth a general theory of money, for presentation to the Diet of 1528." Angus Armitage, <i>The World of Copernicus</i>, 1951, p. 91.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-google12-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-google12_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Watzelrodes—or Watzenrodes—in spite of their rather Germanic name seemed to have been good Poles (enemies of the Teutonic Order)." <li id="cite_note-Moore5262-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Moore5262_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Moore5262_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"[Watzenrode] was also firm, and the Teutonic Knights, who remained a constant menace, did not like him at all; the Grand Master of the order once described him as 'the devil incarnate'. [Watzenrode] was the trusted friend and advisor of three [Polish] kings in succession: John Albert, Alexander (not to be confused with the poisoning pope), and Sigismund; and his influence greatly strengthened the ties between Warmia and Poland proper." Moore (1994), pp. 52, 62.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Davies26-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Davies26_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Davies, Norman (2005). <i>God's playground. <li id="cite_note-Rosen1995_p._127-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_38-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Rosen1995_p._127_38-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Rosen-1995">Rosen</a> (1995, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=C_a1kTvuZ1MC&amp;pg=PA127#v=onepage&amp;f=false">p. 127</a>).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-google19-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-google19_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Although great importance has frequently been ascribed to this fact, it does not imply that Copernicus considered himself to be a German. The 'nationes' of a medieval university had nothing in common with nations in the modern sense of the word. Students who were natives of Prussia and Silesia were automatically described as belonging to the Natio Germanorum. Furthmore, at Bologna, this was the 'privileged' nation; consequently, Copernicus had very good reason for inscribing himself on its register." Koyre, p. 21</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-google20-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-google20_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is important to recognize, however, that the medieval Latin concept of <i>natio</i>, or "nation", referred to the community of feudal lords both in Germany and elsewhere, not to 'the people' in the nineteenth-century democratic or nationalistic sense of the word." Johnson, p.&#160;23.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-google21-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-google21_41-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Koestler, 1968, p. 129.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These interpretations date to the dispute about Copernicus' (Polish vs. German) ethnicity, which had been open since the 1870s, and both the "copper" vs. "dill" interpretations go back to the 19th century, (<i>Magazin für die Literatur des Auslandes</i>, 1875, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1HIDAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA534">534f</a>), but the dispute became virulent once again in the 1960s, culminating in a controversy between E. Mosko ("copper") and <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Rospond" title="Stanisław Rospond">S. Rospond</a> ("dill") during 1963/4, summarized by Zygmunt Brocki, "Wsrôd publikacji o etymologii nazwiska Mikotaja Kopernika [Among the publications on the etymology of the name of Nicholas Copernicus]" <i>Komunikaty mazur.-warm.</i>, 1970).</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Copernicus' brother Andreas would, before the end of 1512, develop <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> and be forced to leave Warmia for Italy. In November 1518 Copernicus would learn that his brother had died. Sobel (2011) pp. 26, 34, 40.</span></li>

Robbins <a href="#Reference-Robbins-1964">(1964, p.x)</a>, however, includes Copernicus among a list of Renaissance astronomers who "either practiced astrology themselves or countenanced its practice".</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-archive-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-archive_84-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Dreyer-1953">Dreyer</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/historyofplaneta00dreyuoft#page/319/mode/1up">(1953, p. 319)</a>.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="#CITEREFBell1992">Bell 1992</a>, p.&#160;111:</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">[…] Copernicus, on his deathbed, received the printer's proofs of his epoch-breaking <i>Dē revolutionibus orbium coelestium</i>.</span></dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Koestler-1963">Koestler 1963</a>, page 189, says the following about a letter from Canon <a href="/wiki/Tiedemann_Giese" title="Tiedemann Giese">Tiedemann Giese</a> to <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joachim_Rheticus" title="Georg Joachim Rheticus">Georg Joachim Rheticus</a>.</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">[…] the end came only after several months, on 24 May. In a letter to Rheticus, written a few weeks later, Giese recorded the event in a single, tragic sentence:</span>
<dl>
<dd><span class="reference-text">For many days he had been deprived of his memory and mental vigour; he only saw his completed book at the last moment, on the day he died.</span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<span class="reference-text">Koestler attributes this quotation to <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Prowe" title="Leopold Prowe">Leopold Prowe</a>, <i>Nicolaus Copernicus</i>, Berlin 1883–1884, Volume 1, part 2, page 554.</span></li>
